Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
Moral Attributes as Necessary Consequences
Buti defines moral attributes as consequences of essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the moral attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyyah) are not independent realities but the necessary logical consequences of the essential attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī).

Core Idea
Moral attributes are the logical outcomes of essential attributes.

In this passage, Buti explains the second category of divine attributes: the moral attributes. He clarifies that these are not separate realities but simply the judgments that follow from the essential attributes like Knowledge and Power. This distinction is important in Sunni theology, especially in contrast to the Mu'tazila who treated them as independent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp Buti's own wording. Notice the phrase 'nothing more than the results'.
Pay attention to the list of attributes that follow.
2
Identify the examples given
Buti lists attributes like Powerful, Willing, Knowing. Each is a consequence of a specific essential attribute (e.g., Power, Will, Knowledge).
This shows the one-to-one relationship.
3
Understand the contrast with Mu'tazila
Buti notes that the Mu'tazila agreed in affirming these attributes to Allah but considered them independent and primary, not as consequences of substantial attributes.
This highlights a key theological difference.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Ask yourself: Why does it matter whether moral attributes are independent or consequential? How does this preserve divine simplicity?
This connects to the broader Aqeedah framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - الصفات المعنوية أما الصفات المعنوية فهي ليست أكثر من نتائج صفات المعاني ، أي هي الأحكام التي تترتب على ثبوت صفات المعاني . فهي كونه جل جلالـه قـديـراً ، مريداً علماً ، سميعاً ، بصيراً ، متكلماً ، حياً ، ولم يخالف المعتزلة في نسبة هذه الصفات بهذا الشكل إلى الله جل جلاله ، ولكنهم حكموا بها استقلالاً وابتداء ودون أن يروا أنها نتيجة صفات ذاتية ثابتة الله عز وجل كما مر بيانه . وليس لنا أي غرض في هذا المقام ببيان شيء أكثر من الذي أوضحناه لك في هذا الصدد
2 - The Moral Attributes As for the moral attributes, they are nothing more than the results of the attributes of meanings, i.e., they are the judgments that follow from the establishment of the attributes of meanings. They are His being Powerful, Willing, Knowing, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, Living. And the Mu'tazila did not disagree in attributing these to Allah (mighty and majestic) in this form, but they judged them as independent and primary, without seeing that they are the result of substantial attributes established in Allah (mighty and majestic) as has been clarified. And we have no purpose in this context to explain more than what we have clarified to you in this regard.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage explains the second category: moral attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyyah). Buti states clearly that these are not separate realities but simply the logical consequences (natāʾij) of the essential attributes (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). For example, from the attribute of Knowledge (ʿilm) it necessarily follows that the essence is 'Knowing' (ʿālim). Similarly, from Power (qudrah) comes 'Powerful' (qādir), etc. These are the judgments (aḥkām) that arise from the essential attributes. He then notes that the Mu'tazila agreed in affirming these to Allah, but they considered them as independent, primary qualifications, without recognizing them as effects of real attributes subsisting in the Divine Essence. He ends by stating he has no need to elaborate further on this point.

Key Terms
الصفات المعنوية (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyyah)
نتائج (natāʾij)
الأحكام (al-aḥkām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the moral attributes are independent realities separate from the essential attributes.
Correction: Buti explicitly states that moral attributes are 'nothing more than the results' of the essential attributes – they are judgments that arise from them, not independent realities.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between the moral attributes and the attributes of meanings according to Buti?
A) The moral attributes are the causes of the attributes of meanings.
B) The moral attributes are the results of the attributes of meanings.
C) They are identical.
D) The moral attributes are independent of the attributes of meanings.

Key Takeaway
The moral attributes are nothing more than the logical consequences of the essential attributes of meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that Allah's moral attributes (like being All-Powerful) are not independent but flow necessarily from His essence. Does your heart truly rely on these attributes as rooted in His eternal essence, or do you sometimes treat them as if they were detached qualities that could change? What would change if you lived with certainty that His Power is inseparable from His very being?
?
The Mu'tazila considered these attributes primary and independent, denying they are results of essential attributes. Is there any virtue or good quality you claim for yourself that you treat as primary and independent, forgetting it is merely a gift flowing from Allah's attributes? How would your humility grow if you saw every good in you as a mere consequence of His perfection?

Divine Attributes
beginner
6 min
Classification of Divine Attributes by Their Objects
Buti classifies divine attributes by their objects into four categories (first two detailed).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti classifies divine attributes into categories based on whether they relate to necessary, possible, and impossible objects, and why will and power are limited to possible beings.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are not all alike: some relate to everything, others only to possible beings.

In this passage, Buti introduces a key distinction: not all divine attributes relate to the same kind of object. Some attributes, like knowledge and speech, encompass the necessary, possible, and impossible. Others, like will and power, only concern possible beings. This classification helps us understand the scope and nature of each attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and English translation to get an overview of Buti's division. Notice that he mentions four categories but only details the first two.
The passage begins with 'These attributes, with regard to their objects, are divided into four categories.'
2
Identify the two categories described
Find the first category: attributes that relate to necessary, possible, and impossible. These are knowledge and speech. Then find the second category: attributes that relate only to possible things: will and power.
Buti explains why each attribute belongs to its category.
3
Understand the reasoning for each category
For knowledge: it simply reveals realities without effect, so it must encompass all objects. For speech: it involves declaration, command, and prohibition, which can address all types. For will and power: they involve specification and effect, which only apply to possible things; the necessary cannot be annihilated and the impossible cannot be created.
4
Reflect on the logical consequence
Consider Buti's argument at the end: if will or power affected the necessary or impossible, it would lead to a contradiction (conjunction of opposites), which is impossible. This shows why these attributes are restricted to possible objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣ - بيان متعلق كل صفة من هذه الصفات تنقسم هذه الصفات بالنظر إلى متعلقاتها إلى أربعة أقسام : فالقسم الأول منها يتعلق بالواجبات والممكنات والمستحيلات جميعاً ، وهو كل من صفتي العلم والكلام . أما صفة العلم فلأنها كما قلنا ، إنما تكشف حقائق الأشياء على ما عليه دون أي تأثير فيها ، ومن المحال أن لا يكون ذلك بالنسبة إليه سبحانه وتعالى متناولاً لسائر الواجبات والممكنات والمستحيلات . وأما صفة الكلام فلأنها تتعلق بالأشياء تعلق دلالة وبيان أو أمر ونهي ، وقد احتوى بيانه سبحانه وتعالى وأمره ونهيه الحديث عن الواجب وعن المستحيل ، وعن الممكن كما تشهد بذلك آيات كتابه الكريم . وأما القسم الثاني منها ، فيتعلق بالممكنات فقط ، وهو كل من صفتي الإرادة والقدرة . أما الواجب والمستحيل فلا شأن لهاتين الصفتين بها . وبيان ذلك أن كلاً من صفتي الإرادة والقدرة إنما يتعلقان بالأشياء على وجه التخصيص والتأثير كالإيجاد والإعدام ونحو ذلك . والواجب لا يمكن إعـدامــه والمستحيل لا يمكن إيجاده ، وإلا لم يكن الواجب واجباً ولا المستحيل مستحيلاً ولو أمكن انعدام الواجب مع بقائه واجباً أو إيجاد المستحيل مع كونه مستحيلاً ، لأمكن اجتماع النقيضين في آن واحد ومكان واحد ، وهو معلوم الاستحالة لكافة العقلاء
3 - Explanation of the Object of Each of These Attributes These attributes, with regard to their objects, are divided into four categories: The first category relates to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible together, and these are the two attributes of knowledge and speech. As for the attribute of knowledge, because it, as we said, merely reveals the realities of things as they are without any effect in them, and it is impossible that it not, with respect to Him (glorified and exalted), encompass all necessary, possible, and impossible things. As for the attribute of speech, because it relates to things as indication and clarification or command and prohibition, and His clarification (glorified and exalted) and His command and prohibition have included discourse about the necessary, the impossible, and the possible, as witnessed by the verses of His Noble Book. As for the second category, it relates only to the possible, and these are the two attributes of will and power. The necessary and the impossible have no relation to these two attributes. And the explanation of that is that each of the two attributes of will and power relate to things in the manner of specification and effect, like bringing into existence and annihilating and the like. The necessary cannot be annihilated and the impossible cannot be brought into existence, otherwise the necessary would not be necessary nor the impossible impossible. And if the annihilation of the necessary while it remains necessary were possible, or the bringing into existence of the impossible while it remains impossible, then the conjunction of contradictories in one moment and one place would be possible, which is known to be impossible to all rational people.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage provides a taxonomy of the divine attributes based on the types of objects they relate to: the necessary (wājib, i.e., Allah Himself and what must be for Him), the possible (mumkin, i.e., created beings), and the impossible (mustaḥīl, i.e., logically impossible). Buti identifies a division into four categories, but only the first two are detailed in this passage. The first category consists of knowledge and speech, which relate to all three types. Knowledge simply reveals realities without effect, and speech expresses meaning, command, or prohibition, as seen in the Quran. The second category comprises will and power, relating only to possibles because they involve specification and effect; the necessary cannot be annihilated and the impossible cannot be created, as that would entail a contradiction. The passage ends with a logical argument against such absurdity.

Key Terms
الواجبات (al-wājibāt)
الممكنات (al-mumkināt)
المستحيلات (al-mustaḥīlāt)
العلم (al-ʿilm)
الكلام (al-kalām)
الإرادة (al-irādah)
القدرة (al-qudrah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes relate to all things equally, without distinction.
Correction: Buti shows that attributes like knowledge and speech relate to necessary, possible, and impossible, while will and power only relate to possible beings because they involve effect and change.

Checkpoint
Which attributes relate to necessary, possible, and impossible objects according to Buti's classification?
A) Will and Power.
B) Knowledge and Speech.
C) Life and Hearing.
D) All attributes.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge and speech encompass all things, while will and power are limited to possible beings due to their nature as effecting change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's knowledge encompasses every detail of your life, including your hidden thoughts and future decisions? If so, why do you still worry about what is not in your control? Commit today to surrender one specific anxiety to His all-encompassing knowledge.
?
The attribute of will and power only apply to possible beings. Does this challenge your assumptions about what Allah can and cannot do? For example, do you sometimes doubt that He can change your situation because it seems 'impossible'? How does this lesson reshape your understanding of what is possible for Allah to will and do?
?
If you truly internalized that divine will only relates to possible beings, would you still make excuses for not changing by saying 'Allah willed it' for something you could actually change? What is one area of your life where you blame divine will for your own inaction, and what will you do about it now?

Divine Attributes

The First Category: Knowledge and Speech Encompass All Realities
Buti defines the first category of attributes and justifies their universal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge and speech are attributes that relate to all three modal categories: the necessary, the possible, and the impossible.

Core Idea
Knowledge and speech disclose and communicate all realities without affecting them.

Buti begins his classification of divine attributes by identifying those that are universal in their scope. Knowledge and speech are such attributes because they do not involve causative influence but rather disclosure and communication. This lesson explains how these two attributes encompass everything, including the necessary, possible, and impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the two attributes mentioned.
Focus on the reasoning given for each.
2
Understand knowledge's scope
Buti argues that knowledge discloses realities without effect, so it must cover all things. Why can't it exclude anything?
This includes impossibilities because they are known as impossible.
3
Understand speech's scope
Speech indicates or commands/prohibits. The Quran shows God speaking about all three categories.
4
Reflect on implications
How does this understanding affect our concept of God's omniscience and communication?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنقسم هذه الصفات بالنظر إلى متعلقاتها إلى أربعة أقسام :

فالقسم الأول منها يتعلق بالواجبات والممكنات والمستحيلات جميعاً ، وهو كل من صفتي العلم والكلام . أما صفة العلم فلأنها كما قلنا ، إنما تكشف حقائق الأشياء على ما عليه دون أي تأثير فيها ، ومن المحال أن لا يكون ذلك بالنسبة إليه سبحانه وتعالى متناولاً لسائر الواجبات والممكنات والمستحيلات . وأما صفة الكلام فلأنها تتعلق بالأشياء تعلق دلالة وبيان أو أمر ونهي ، وقد احتوى بيانه سبحانه وتعالى وأمره ونهيه الحديث عن الواجب وعن المستحيل ، وعن الممكن كما تشهد بذلك آيات كتابه الكريم .

وأما القسم الثاني منها ، فيتعلق بالممكنات فقط ، وهو كل من صفتي الإرادة والقدرة . أما الواجب والمستحيل فلا شأن لهاتين الصفتين بها .

وبيان ذلك أن كلاً من صفتي الإرادة والقدرة إنما يتعلقان بالأشياء على وجه التخصيص والتأثير كالإيجاد والإعدام ونحو ذلك . والواجب لا يمكن إعدامه والمستحيل لا يمكن إيجاده ، وإلا لم يكن الواجب واجباً ولا المستحيل مستحيلاً . ولو أمكن انعدام الواجب مع بقائه واجباً أو إيجاد المستحيل مع كونه مستحيلاً ، لأمكن اجتماع النقيضين في آن واحد ومكان واحد ، وهو معلوم الاستحالة لكافة العقلاء .
These attributes are divided by consideration of their objects into four sections: The first section pertains to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible all together, and it is both attributes of knowledge and speech. As for the attribute of knowledge, because it, as we have said, only discloses the realities of things as they are without any effect on them, and it is impossible that it should not, with respect to Him, Exalted and Majestic, encompass all necessary, possible, and impossible things. As for the attribute of speech, because it relates to things as indication and clarification or command and prohibition, and His clarification, Exalted and Majestic, and His command and His prohibition have contained speech about the necessary, about the impossible, and about the possible, as the verses of His Noble Book bear witness. As for the second section, it pertains only to the possible, and it is both attributes of will and power. The necessary and the impossible have no concern with these two attributes. The explanation of that is that each of the two attributes of will and power only relate to things in the mode of specification and effect, such as bringing into existence and annihilating and the like. The necessary cannot be annihilated, and the impossible cannot be brought into existence, otherwise the necessary would not be necessary nor the impossible impossible. And if it were possible to annihilate the necessary while it remains necessary, or to bring the impossible into existence while it is impossible, then the two contradictories could meet at one time and one place, and that is known to be impossible by all rational persons.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage classifies divine attributes based on their objects (متعلقات). Buti states that there are four categories, then explains the first two in detail. The first category includes knowledge (علم) and speech (كلام), which relate to all three modal categories: the necessary (واجب), the possible (ممكن), and the impossible (مستحيل). Knowledge discloses realities without affecting them, so its object is unrestricted. Speech indicates or commands/prohibits, and the Qur'an confirms God's speech about all three modes. The second category includes will (إرادة) and power (قدرة), which relate only to possibles because they involve specification and effect (such as creation and annihilation). The necessary cannot be annihilated, and the impossible cannot be created; if they could, a contradiction would arise (two contradictories simultaneously), which is rationally impossible. The argument flows from the nature of these attributes and the logical absurdity of applying will/power to what is necessarily existent or impossible.

Key Terms
علم
كلام
واجب
ممكن
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine knowledge does not cover impossible things because they are not actual.
Correction: Buti argues that knowledge discloses realities as they are; thus, it includes the impossibility of things like a partner to God.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, why does divine knowledge encompass impossible things?
A) Because knowledge can change impossibilities.
B) Because knowledge discloses realities without effect, so it includes all things.
C) Because impossible things can become possible.
D) Because knowledge is limited to actual things.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and speech are universal attributes that relate to all things, including the necessary, possible, and impossible, because they are non-effectual and communicative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge discloses even the impossible realities—things that will never happen—what does that mean for the sinful thoughts and intentions you assume are hidden? How will you live today knowing that nothing escapes His all-encompassing knowledge?
?
God's speech has already addressed the necessary (what you must believe and do), the impossible (what you must avoid), and the possible (your choices). Are you treating His command and prohibition as covering all areas of your life, or are there 'possible' areas you have excluded from His authority? What specific area will you surrender to Him today?
?
The passage says divine knowledge has no effect on the things it discloses—it simply knows them as they are. Does your belief in God's knowledge change how you see yourself? If He knows your weaknesses and failures without changing them, will you stop hiding and instead turn to Him with full honesty?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
The Second Category: Will and Power Limited to Possibles
Buti defines the second category and provides a rational proof for its limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will and power can only relate to possible things and not to the necessary or impossible.

Core Idea
Will and power involve effect, so they logically apply only to what can be caused.

After covering the universal attributes, Buti turns to those that are selective. Will and power are attributes of specification and effect; they bring things into existence or annihilate them. Therefore, they cannot be directed towards what is already necessary (cannot be annihilated) or what is impossible (cannot be created). This lesson unpacks the logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on second category
Read the Arabic passage and note the two attributes and their restriction.
The argument uses logic about contradictories.
2
Understand the nature of will and power
Buti says they relate to things with specification and effect.
3
Why necessary and impossible are excluded
The necessary cannot be annihilated, the impossible cannot be created. If it were possible, it would lead to a contradiction.
Think about what would happen if God annihilated Himself (impossible).
4
Appreciate the logical conclusion
Therefore, will and power only apply to possibles, preserving God's perfection and logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنقسم هذه الصفات بالنظر إلى متعلقاتها إلى أربعة أقسام :

فالقسم الأول منها يتعلق بالواجبات والممكنات والمستحيلات جميعاً ، وهو كل من صفتي العلم والكلام . أما صفة العلم فلأنها كما قلنا ، إنما تكشف حقائق الأشياء على ما عليه دون أي تأثير فيها ، ومن المحال أن لا يكون ذلك بالنسبة إليه سبحانه وتعالى متناولاً لسائر الواجبات والممكنات والمستحيلات . وأما صفة الكلام فلأنها تتعلق بالأشياء تعلق دلالة وبيان أو أمر ونهي ، وقد احتوى بيانه سبحانه وتعالى وأمره ونهيه الحديث عن الواجب وعن المستحيل ، وعن الممكن كما تشهد بذلك آيات كتابه الكريم .

وأما القسم الثاني منها ، فيتعلق بالممكنات فقط ، وهو كل من صفتي الإرادة والقدرة . أما الواجب والمستحيل فلا شأن لهاتين الصفتين بها .

وبيان ذلك أن كلاً من صفتي الإرادة والقدرة إنما يتعلقان بالأشياء على وجه التخصيص والتأثير كالإيجاد والإعدام ونحو ذلك . والواجب لا يمكن إعدامه والمستحيل لا يمكن إيجاده ، وإلا لم يكن الواجب واجباً ولا المستحيل مستحيلاً . ولو أمكن انعدام الواجب مع بقائه واجباً أو إيجاد المستحيل مع كونه مستحيلاً ، لأمكن اجتماع النقيضين في آن واحد ومكان واحد ، وهو معلوم الاستحالة لكافة العقلاء .
These attributes are divided by consideration of their objects into four sections: The first section pertains to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible all together, and it is both attributes of knowledge and speech. As for the attribute of knowledge, because it, as we have said, only discloses the realities of things as they are without any effect on them, and it is impossible that it should not, with respect to Him, Exalted and Majestic, encompass all necessary, possible, and impossible things. As for the attribute of speech, because it relates to things as indication and clarification or command and prohibition, and His clarification, Exalted and Majestic, and His command and His prohibition have contained speech about the necessary, about the impossible, and about the possible, as the verses of His Noble Book bear witness. As for the second section, it pertains only to the possible, and it is both attributes of will and power. The necessary and the impossible have no concern with these two attributes. The explanation of that is that each of the two attributes of will and power only relate to things in the mode of specification and effect, such as bringing into existence and annihilating and the like. The necessary cannot be annihilated, and the impossible cannot be brought into existence, otherwise the necessary would not be necessary nor the impossible impossible. And if it were possible to annihilate the necessary while it remains necessary, or to bring the impossible into existence while it is impossible, then the two contradictories could meet at one time and one place, and that is known to be impossible by all rational persons.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage classifies divine attributes based on their objects (متعلقات). Buti states that there are four categories, then explains the first two in detail. The first category includes knowledge (علم) and speech (كلام), which relate to all three modal categories: the necessary (واجب), the possible (ممكن), and the impossible (مستحيل). Knowledge discloses realities without affecting them, so its object is unrestricted. Speech indicates or commands/prohibits, and the Qur'an confirms God's speech about all three modes. The second category includes will (إرادة) and power (قدرة), which relate only to possibles because they involve specification and effect (such as creation and annihilation). The necessary cannot be annihilated, and the impossible cannot be created; if they could, a contradiction would arise (two contradictories simultaneously), which is rationally impossible. The argument flows from the nature of these attributes and the logical absurdity of applying will/power to what is necessarily existent or impossible.

Key Terms
إرادة
قدرة
واجب
ممكن
مستحيل
نقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that because God is all-powerful, He can annihilate Himself or create a stone so big He can't lift it.
Correction: Buti's argument shows that such actions are logically impossible and therefore not objects of power.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, why cannot will and power apply to the necessary?
A) Because the necessary does not need will.
B) Because the necessary cannot be annihilated, as it is necessarily existent.
C) Because God does not will the necessary.
D) Because the necessary is not an object.

Key Takeaway
Will and power are selective attributes that only relate to possible things, because applying them to the necessary or impossible would lead to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine you have persistently begged Allah for something that is logically impossible (e.g., to see Him in this world). What does this lesson reveal about the wisdom behind Allah not answering that request? How does it reshape your understanding of His will and power?
?
If will and power only relate to possible things, then what does that mean for the sincerity of your duas? Are you ever guilty of asking Allah for things you know are impossible, treating them as mere wishes? How can this lesson purify your supplication and deepen your reliance on Allah's wisdom?
?
Reflect on a time you struggled with the idea that Allah could do 'anything' — including what defies logic. Does this lesson make you more or less trusting of Him? Why? What does the logical coherence of His attributes reveal about His perfection?

Restriction of Will and Power to Possibles Does Not Imply Weakness
Buti refutes the objection that limiting will and power to possibles implies weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fact that God's will and power only attach to possibles is not a sign of weakness, but rather a necessary consequence of their perfection.

Core Idea
Not attaching to impossibles is not weakness; it is the very meaning of perfect will and power.

Some might think that if God cannot will or do the impossible, that means He is limited and imperfect. Buti explains that this objection misunderstands what will and power are. In this passage, he demonstrates that perfect will and power are naturally directed only toward possibles, and that demanding they attach to impossibles is as meaningless as 'annihilating the non-existent.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the flow: Buti states his thesis, gives a concrete example, then an analogy.
2
Identify the objection and its refutation
What is the objection? That limiting will/power to possibles means weakness. How does Buti refute it? He says it's not weakness but the nature of perfect will. Follow his reasoning.
3
Analyze the example of creating a partner
Buti gives a specific example: if God's will attached to creating a partner (impossible), that leads to contradiction. Work through the logic step by step.
4
Understand the analogy of annihilation
Buti uses the analogy of 'annihilating the non-existent' to show that some acts are inherently meaningless. This clarifies that the impossibility of an act does not indicate deficiency in the agent.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلق الإرادة والقدرة بالممكنات وحدها لا يعني العجز :

وإذا أمعنت النظر في معنى هذا الكلام، علمت أن تعلق الإرادة والقدرة بالممكنات فقط ، لا يعني العجز أو نقصان الإرادة . وإنما يعني أن الإرادة الكاملة التامة ليس من شأنها أن تتجه إلى الواجب ما دام أنه واجب ، أو إلى المستحيل ما دام مستحيلاً ، وكذلك القدرة . بل لا يمكن للعقل أن يفهم كيف تتعلق الإرادة أو القدرة بالواجب أو المستحيل . فلو قيل مثلاً : إن إرادة الله تعلقت بإيجاد المستحيل ) وهو الشريك في الألوهية ( فأوجدته - فإن عقلك لا يمكن أن يصدق إطلاقاً هذا الكلام ، لأنه مستحيل بالبداهة .
إذ معنى هذا القول أنه قد أوجد إلهاً مثله واجب الوجود ، وواجب الوجود يستحيل أن يكون مسبوقاً بعدم ، كما بينا من قبل ، وإذاً فليس هو في الحقيقة واجب الوجود . وإن قلت بل هو كذلك على الرغم من أنه مخلوق ومسبوق بعدم ، فمعنى ذلك أنك تقول : إنه واجب الوجود رغم أنه ممكن الوجود وهو تناقض صريح يلفظه العقل .

فهذا معنى قولنا إنَّ عدم تعلق الإرادة والقدرة بغير الممكن لا يسمى عجزاً أو نقصاناً . وإنما هو لأن معناهما قائم على عدم التعلق بغير الممكن . كالإعدام مثلاً فإنه لا يمكن أن يظهر أثره إلا إذا تعلق بالموجود أما المعدوم بطبيعته فإن معنى الإعدام لا يمكن أن يتعلق به وليس ذلك دليلاً على نقص هذا المعنى أو ضعفه بحال . وإن رحت تكرهه على التعلق به بموجب الصياغة اللفظية مثلاً ، كقولك : أعدمت المعدوم ، فإنك لا تفعل بذلك أكثر من أن تؤلف كلاماً فارغاً لا معنى له .

وإنك لترى في الناس نماذج من المتهوسين ، يحسبون أن بإمكانهم زعزعة الإيمان بالله في قلوب طائفة من المؤمنين . إذا ما جابهوهم بهذا السؤال : هل
The attachment of will and power to possibles alone does not mean incapacity: And if you deeply consider the meaning of this statement, you will know that the attachment of will and power only to possibles does not mean incapacity or deficiency of will. Rather, it means that perfect and complete will is not of its nature to turn to the necessary as long as it is necessary, nor to the impossible as long as it is impossible, and likewise power. Rather, the intellect cannot understand how will or power could attach to the necessary or the impossible. So if, for example, it were said: 'God's will attached to bringing the impossible into existence' - that is, a partner in divinity - and He brought it into existence, then your intellect could absolutely not believe this statement, because it is impossible by intuition. For the meaning of this statement is that He has brought into existence a god like Him, necessarily existent, and the necessarily existent cannot be preceded by non-existence, as we have explained before, and therefore it is not in reality necessarily existent. And if you say, 'Rather it is so despite being created and preceded by non-existence,' then that means you are saying it is necessarily existent even though it is possibly existent, which is an explicit contradiction that the intellect rejects. So this is the meaning of our statement that the non-attachment of will and power to other than the possible is not called incapacity or deficiency. Rather, it is because their meaning is based on non-attachment to other than the possible. Like annihilation, for example: it cannot show its effect unless it attaches to something existing. As for that which is non-existent by nature, the meaning of annihilation cannot attach to it, and that is not evidence of deficiency or weakness of this meaning at all. And if you force it to attach to it by verbal formulation, such as your saying 'I annihilated the non-existent,' you do nothing more than compose empty speech with no meaning. And indeed you see among people types of the obsessed who think they can shake faith in God in the hearts of a group of believers when they confront them with this question: 'Is...'
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection: that restricting divine will and power to possibles only implies weakness in God. Buti argues that it does not imply weakness; rather, perfect will and power are not directed toward the necessary or the impossible because those are inherently beyond their scope. He gives a concrete example: if someone claimed God willed to create a partner (an impossible thing), that would entail a contradiction (a necessary being that is created and preceded by non-existence). Such a statement is rationally impossible. He then analogizes with the concept of 'annihilating the non-existent' – a meaningless verbal formulation that does not reflect any real capacity. The point is that the impossibility of an act is not due to lack of power but due to the nature of the act itself. Finally, he mentions that some obsessed people use such sophistical questions to shake the faith of believers, introducing a question that will be addressed (possibly on the next page). The core argument is that the non-attachment of will and power to the impossible is inherent in their meaning, not a sign of deficiency.

The Scope of Divine Will and Power
Explains that divine will and power only attach to possible things, not a deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attributes of will and power only attach to possible things, and why this does not indicate any deficiency or weakness.

Core Idea
Divine will and power are perfect precisely because they are confined to what is possible.

This passage addresses a common theological concern: if God cannot bring about logically impossible things, does that mean He is not all-powerful? The author explains that the very meaning of will and power precludes their attachment to the necessary or impossible. Through a logical example and a vivid analogy, he shows that such non-attachment is a sign of perfection, not limitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author starts with a clear thesis and builds his argument step by step.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence that states that non-attachment to impossible things is not deficiency. Understand what the author is trying to prove.
3
Follow the example of the impossible partner
Work through the hypothetical example of creating a partner for God. See how the author shows it leads to a logical contradiction.
This illustrates why will and power cannot attach to impossible things.
4
Grasp the analogy of 'making non-existent'
Consider the analogy of i'daam (annihilation). Just as it cannot apply to something already non-existent, divine attributes cannot apply to impossible things. Understand why this does not imply weakness.
This solidifies the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلق الإرادة والقدرة بالممكنات وحدها لا يعني العجز :
وإذا أمعنت النظر في معنى هذا الكلام، علمت أن تعلق الإرادة والقدرة بالممكنات فقط ، لا يعني العجز أو نقصان الإرادة . وإنما يعني أن الإرادة الكاملة التامة ليس من شأنها أن تتجه إلى الواجب ما دام أنه واجب ، أو إلى المستحيل ما دام مستحيلاً ، وكذلك القدرة . بل لا يمكن للعقل أن يفهم كيف تتعلق الإرادة أو القدرة بالواجب أو المستحيل . فلو قيل مثلاً : إن إرادة الله تعلقت بإيجاد المستحيل ( وهو الشريك في الألوهية ) فأوجدته - فإن عقلك لا يمكن أن يصدق إطلاقاً هذا الكلام ، لأنه مستحيل بالبداهة
إذ معنى هذا القول أنه قد أوجد إلها مثله واجب الوجود ، وواجب الوجود يستحيل أن يكون مسبوقاً بعدم ، كما بينا من قبل ، وإذاً فليس هو في الحقيقة واجب الوجود . وإن قلت بل هو كذلك على الرغم من أنه مخلوق ومسبوق بعدم ، فمعنى ذلك أنك تقول : إنه واجب الوجود رغم أنه ممكن الوجود وهو تناقض صريح يلفظه العقل

فهذا معنى قولنا إنَّ عدم تعلق الإرادة والقدرة بغير الممكن لا يسمى عجزاً أو نقصاناً . وإنما هو لأن معناهما قائم على عدم التعلق بغير الممكن . كالإعدام مثلاً فإنه لا يمكن أن يظهر أثره إلا إذا تعلق بالموجود أما المعدوم بطبيعته فإن معنى الإعدام لا يمكن أن يتعلق به وليس ذلك دليلاً على نقص هذا المعنى أو ضعفه بحال . وإن رحت تكرهه على التعلق به بموجب الصياغة اللفظية مثلاً ، كقولك : أعدمت المعدوم ، فإنك لا تفعل بذلك أكثر من أن تؤلف كلاماً فارغاً لا معنى له
The attachment of will and power to possible things alone does not mean incapacity:

And if you deeply consider the meaning of this statement, you know that the attachment of will and power to possible things only does not mean incapacity or deficiency of will. Rather it means that the perfect complete will is not such that it turns to the necessary as long as it is necessary, or to the impossible as long as it is impossible, and likewise power. Rather the intellect cannot understand how will or power could attach to the necessary or the impossible. For example, if it were said: 'Indeed God's will attached to bringing into existence the impossible (which is a partner in divinity) and He brought it into existence' – your intellect cannot believe this statement at all, because it is impossible by necessity (badaha).

For the meaning of this statement is that He has brought into existence a god like Him, necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud), and the necessary of existence is impossible to be preceded by non-existence, as we have explained before. Thus it is not in truth necessary of existence. And if you say: 'Rather it is so despite being created and preceded by non-existence,' then the meaning is that you say: 'It is necessary of existence despite being possible of existence' – and this is a clear contradiction that the intellect rejects.

So this is the meaning of our statement that the non-attachment of will and power to other than the possible is not called incapacity or deficiency. Rather it is because their meaning is based on non-attachment to other than the possible. Like 'non-existence-making' (i'daam) for example: it cannot show its effect except when it attaches to something existing. As for the non-existent by its nature, the meaning of non-existence-making cannot attach to it, and that is not evidence of deficiency of this meaning or its weakness at all. If you try to force it to attach to it by means of verbal formulation, for example, like your saying: 'I made the non-existent non-existent' (a'damtu al-ma'dum), then you do nothing more than compose empty speech that has no meaning.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of the divine attributes of will (al-iraada) and power (al-qudra), and refutes the misconception that their limitation to possible things (al-mumkinat) implies deficiency or weakness. The author argues that it is logically necessary for these attributes to only relate to possible things, because the necessary (al-wajib) and the impossible (al-mustahil) are, by definition, beyond their scope. He illustrates with an example: if one were to claim that God brought into existence a partner (a second god), that would be contradictory because such a being would have to be both necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud) and created (makhluq), which is impossible. The author then uses the analogy of 'making non-existent' (i'daam): the act of annihilating can only apply to something that exists; applying it to something non-existent is meaningless. Thus, non-attachment to impossible things is not a deficiency but is inherent to the meaning of these attributes.

Correction: The passage shows that divine will and power, by their very nature, only relate to possible things. Non-attachment to impossible things is not a deficiency but a logical necessity inherent in the meaning of these attributes.

The Question 'Can God Create Another God' Is Nonsense
Refutes the atheistic objection about God creating another god as meaningless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'Can God create another god like Himself' is logically meaningless and does not deserve a response.

Core Idea
A question that contradicts itself is not a question—it is nonsense.

Atheists sometimes ask whether God can create another god, hoping to trap believers. But this question is not as clever as it seems. In fact, it is self-contradictory and has no real meaning. In this passage, the author exposes the logical flaw and shows why such questions should be disregarded.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Notice the author's tone and the flow of the argument.
The passage uses an analogy to clarify its point.
2
Understand the motive behind the objection
The author explains why atheists ask this question: they think it forces a dilemma—either admit polytheists are not disbelievers or admit God is incapable.
This sets up why the question is dangerous if taken seriously.
3
Grasp the condition for a valid question
The author states that a real question must have a coherent meaning. If it lacks meaning, it is not a real question but nonsense (hadhayan).
The question about creating another god is self-contradictory because a 'created god' is an impossibility.
4
Reflect on the analogy and conclusion
The author uses the analogy of being both absent and present simultaneously. Such a question has no answer because it has no meaning. Similarly, the atheist's question should be ignored.
The conclusion is that we should not engage with nonsense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنك لترى في الناس نماذج من المتهوسين ، يحسبون أن بإمكانهم زعزعة الإيمان بالله في قلوب طائفة من المؤمنين ، إذا ما جابهوهم بهذا السؤال : هل يستطيع الله أن يخلق إلهاً مثله ؟ تصوراً منهم بأن المسؤولين إذا أجابوا بالإمكان اعترفوا بذلك أنه ليس لهم أن يكفّروا من أشرك مع الله غيره ، وإن أجابوا بعدم الإمكان فقد أسندوا إلى الله العجز وذلك دليل على أنه ليس باله ! ...

وهذا التصور يعود في حقيقته إلى حمق من نوع عجيب ! ...

فمن المعلوم أن السائل - لكي يعتبر سائلاً في الحقيقة - ينبغي أن يتصور معنى سؤاله ، ولكي يتصور معناه ينبغي أن يكون له معنى : فأما إذا لم يكن للسؤال معنى فلا يمكن أن يكون له صورة في ذهن السائل، وإذا كان كذلك ، فإن السؤال لا يكون حينئذ سؤالاً إلا من حيث الصورة والأسلوب ، وأما من حيث يسمى الموضوع والمضمون فهو هذيان ، والهذيان لا جواب عليه ، لا عجزاً عن الإجابة ، ولكن لأن الإجابة لا تكون إلا على سؤال والسؤال لم يولد في الحقيقة بعد

إن الذي يقول لك : هل تستطيع أن تكون في هذه اللحظة غائباً مشاهداً أمامي ؟ - هو في الحقيقة لا يقدم لك أي سؤال أو رجاء يطلب الإجابة نفسه لا يعلم ما يريد بالضبط وليس في ذهنه أي صورة لهذا الذي يريد ، ومحال أن يكون في ذهنه صورة لمعنى هذا السؤال ، فمهما كان الجواب - على فرض أن يكون له جواب - فإنه لا يقع مطابقاً لأي معنى متخيل في ذهن [؟] السائل عليه ، لأنه

وبتعبير أوضح نقول ، إن هذا السؤال ليس في جوهره إلا هذياناً ليس من فرق بينه وبين أي جملة من الكلام المختلط الذي لا معنى له ، فمن الطبيعي أن تنظر بإشفاق تام إلى من يستوقفك ليضع لك هذياناً بصيغة سؤال ، ثم تشيح بوجهك عنه دون أن تجيبه بكلمة . ذلك لأنه لم يقل شيئاً يحتاج إلى جواب ، حتى ينتظر العاقل منك جواباً عليه

إن الذي يستوقفك ليقول لك : هل يستطيع الله أن يخلق إلها مثله ، أو ...
And you see among people examples of the deluded, who think that they can shake faith in God in the hearts of a group of believers, when they confront them with this question: 'Is God able to create a god like Him?' – imagining that if the respondents answer with possibility, they thereby admit that they cannot declare polytheists as disbelievers, and if they answer with impossibility, they have attributed incapacity to God, and that is evidence that He is not God! ...

And this notion in reality returns to a strange kind of foolishness! ...

For it is known that the questioner – in order to be considered a questioner in reality – should conceive the meaning of his question, and in order to conceive its meaning, it should have a meaning. But if the question has no meaning, then it cannot have an image in the mind of the questioner. And if that is the case, then the question is not a question except in terms of form and style, but in terms of its subject and content it is nonsense (hadhayan), and nonsense cannot be answered – not out of inability to answer, but because an answer is only given to a question, and the question has not actually been born.

Indeed, the one who says to you: 'Can you at this moment be absent and present before me?' – he in reality does not present any question or request that demands an answer; he himself does not know exactly what he wants, and there is no image in his mind of what he wants. And it is impossible for there to be in his mind an image of the meaning of this question. So whatever the answer – assuming it has an answer – it does not correspond to any meaning imagined in the mind of the questioner [؟] upon him, because

And in clearer terms, we say: This question is in its essence nothing but nonsense; there is no difference between it and any sentence of confused speech that has no meaning. So it is natural that you look with complete pity at someone who stops you to present nonsense in the form of a question, then you turn your face away from him without answering him with a word. That is because he has not said anything that needs an answer, such that a rational person would expect an answer from you.

Indeed, the one who stops you to say: 'Is God able to create a god like Him,' or ...
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a common atheistic objection that attempts to embarrass believers by asking whether God can create another god like Himself. The author first describes the motives of those who ask this question: they think that if believers answer 'yes', they undermine their own grounds for declaring polytheists disbelievers, and if they answer 'no', they attribute incapacity to God. The author then exposes the question as fundamentally flawed. He argues that a genuine question must have a coherent meaning that can be conceived by the questioner. This question, however, is self-contradictory: to ask for a 'created god' is to ask for something that is both necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud) and possible (mumkin), which is impossible. Therefore, the question is not a real question but mere nonsense (hadhayan). The author illustrates with an analogy: 'Can you be absent and present at the same time?' Such a question has no coherent meaning, so no answer is possible or required. He concludes that one should simply disregard such nonsense, as it does not warrant a response.

Correction: The question is self-contradictory and meaningless; it does not warrant an answer, so the inability to answer does not reflect on God's power.

Key Takeaway

The Objects of Divine Hearing and Sight: Only Existents
Explaining that divine hearing/sight relate only to existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attributes of hearing and sight relate only to existents and not to non-existents, and the scholarly consensus on this matter.

Core Idea
Divine hearing and sight cannot relate to non-existents—that would be a logical contradiction.

This passage from Buti explains the third category of divine attributes: hearing and sight. After establishing that power and will relate only to possibles, Buti turns to hearing and sight. He clarifies that these attributes, like the others, are directed solely to existents. The discussion includes a consensus that non-existents are not objects of divine perception, and he provides a simple analogy to illustrate the impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the author's argument. Notice how he first classifies the attributes, then presents two views, and finally asserts the consensus.
The passage connects hearing and sight to the earlier discussion of power and will.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the sentence that states the consensus: 'these two attributes do not relate to non-existents by consensus'. Understand the reasoning that follows.
3
Examine the analogy
Buti gives an analogy with touch, taste, and smell. Reflect on why claiming to perceive a non-existent is absurd. This reinforces the logical impossibility.
The analogy is meant to appeal to common sense.
4
Connect to earlier lessons
Buti explicitly links this discussion to power and will. Notice how all divine attributes have a consistent principle: they relate only to what is possible or existent, not to impossibilities or non-existents.
This shows the systematic nature of 'ilm al-kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the third category: it relates to existents. And it includes both the attributes of hearing and sight. So they do not relate to non-existents, but rather relate to what is beyond that of the various existents, whether of the type of the possible or the necessary—if we say that each of the attributes of hearing and sight relate to all existents in a comprehensive manner, as an addition to knowledge. But if we go to what al-Sa‘d went to in Sharh al-‘Aqa’id, that hearing only relates to audible things and sight to visible things, then they do not relate to all existents in that case. And we have already mentioned delegating the reality in this matter to Allah Almighty, as many imams and researchers inclined to that, and it suffices us to affirm what Allah Almighty affirmed for Himself. As for what is beyond that for which we have no report or explanation, we consign its knowledge to Allah Almighty. But what is important here is that you know that these two attributes do not relate to non-existents by consensus. For it is not reasonable that they relate to them. Otherwise they would be from the category of existents, and existence and non-existence cannot coexist together because it is a contradiction, and it is impossible. And similar is touch, taste, and smell: do we say 'I touched a non-existent' or 'I tasted it' or 'I smelled it'? And if a person claims that, can any rational person believe him? And know that this research reflects further clarification on the discussion of power and will and their non-relation to other than possibles. For the principle in them is one.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the attributes of hearing and sight (السمع والبصر) as the third category of divine attributes, explaining their objects. The author first states that these attributes relate to existents (الموجودات), not non-existents. He then presents two views: one that they encompass all existents comprehensively, another (attributed to al-Sa‘d in Sharh al-‘Aqa’id) that hearing relates only to audible things and sight to visible things. The author then delegates the precise reality to Allah, following many scholars. The key point is the consensus that these attributes do not relate to non-existents, because that would be a contradiction (existence and non-existence cannot coexist). He gives an analogy: one cannot say 'I touched a non-existent' because it is inconceivable. Finally, he connects this to the earlier discussion of power and will, which also do not relate to impossible things.

The Attribute of Life: No External Object
Sanusi explains the unique nature of the attribute of life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of life is unique among divine attributes in having no external relation and how it serves as the foundation for other attributes.

Core Idea
Life is the foundation that makes all other divine attributes possible.

In this passage, the author distinguishes the attribute of life from other divine attributes by emphasizing that it does not relate to anything outside God's essence. Unlike knowledge, will, and power, which have objects, life is purely inherent. This clarifies the foundational role of life in God.

Guided Path

2

The passage explicitly contrasts life with both types.
3
Understand the function of life

Life is a prerequisite, not an active relation.

This passage defines the fourth category of divine attributes: the attribute of life (الحياة). It is a quality that subsists in God's essence itself, and its role is to make it possible for the other attributes to exist in Him. Thus life is foundational: without life, the other attributes could not be affirmed of God.

The Domain of Divine Hearing and Sight
Buti on the objects of divine hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine hearing and sight can only pertain to existents and not to non-existents, and the scholarly differences on the scope of these attributes.

Core Idea
Divine hearing and sight are limited to existents; non-existents are excluded by logical necessity.

In this passage, Buti clarifies the third category of divine attributes—hearing and sight. He establishes that these attributes, like all divine attributes, have a specific domain: they relate only to existents. The discussion includes two theological positions on whether they encompass all existents or only certain types. Buti also connects this to the earlier discussion on power and will, reinforcing the principle that divine attributes pertain only to possible existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: first the rule, then the two positions, then the proof by contradiction.
2
Identify the two positions
Buti presents two views on the objects of hearing and sight. What are they? Which one does he lean towards?
He delegates the exact reality to Allah.
3
Understand the logical proof
The key argument is that if these attributes pertained to non-existents, it would require coexistence of existence and non-existence, which is impossible. Think about why this is a contradiction.
The analogy with human senses helps clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : فيتعلق بالموجودات . وهو كل من صفتي السمع والبصر . فهما لا يتعلقان بالمعدومات، وإنما يتعلقان بما وراء ذلك من مختلف الموجودات ، سواء كانت من نوع الممكن أم الواجب هذا إن قلنا إن كلاً من صفتي السمع والبصر يتعلقان بالموجودات كلها على وجه الإحاطة تعلقاً زائداً على العلم . أما إذا ذهبنا إلى مثل ما ذهب إليه السعد في شرح العقائد من أن السمع إنما يتعلق بالمسموعات ، والبصر يتعلق بالمبصرات فلا تتعلقان بكل الموجودات حينئذ . وقد سبق أن ذكرنا تفويض الحقيقة في هذا الأمر إلى الله عز وجل كما جنح إلى ذلك كثير من الأئمة والباحثين ، وحسبنا أن نثبت ما أثبته الله تعالى لنفسه . أما ما وراء ذلك مما لم يأتنا خبر عنه وبيان له ، فنكل علمه إلى الله عز وجل غير أن المهم هنا أن تعلم بأن هاتين الصفتين لا تتعلقان بالمعدومات بالاتفاق . إذ لا يعقل تعلقها بها . وإلا لكانت من قسم الموجودات ، ولا يمكن اجتماع الوجود والعدم معاً لأنه تناقض ، وهو محال . ومثل ذلك اللمس والذوق والشم ، أفنقول : لمست المعدوم أو ذقته أو شممته ؟! وإذا ادعى ذلك شخص أفيمكن أن يصدقه عاقل من الناس ؟ واعلم أن هذا البحث يعكس مزيداً من الإيضاح على بحث القدرة والإرادة وعدم تعلقها بغير الممكنات . إذ المبدأ فيهما واحد
As for the third division: it pertains to existents. It is each of the two attributes of hearing and sight. Thus they do not pertain to non-existents; rather they pertain to that which is beyond that of various existents, whether of the type of possible or necessary, if we say that each of the two attributes of hearing and sight pertain to all existents comprehensively in a manner additional to knowledge. But if we adopt what al-Sa'd adopted in his commentary on al-`Aqa'id, namely that hearing pertains only to sounds, and sight pertains only to visible things, then they do not pertain to all existents at that point. And we have previously mentioned consigning the reality of this matter to Allah Almighty, as many of the imams and researchers leaned toward; and it suffices us to affirm what Allah has affirmed for Himself. As for what lies beyond that of which no report or explanation has come to us, we consign its knowledge to Allah Almighty. However, the important thing here is that you know that these two attributes do not pertain to non-existents by consensus, since it is inconceivable for them to pertain to them. Otherwise they would be of the division of existents, and it is impossible for existence and non-existence to coexist because that is a contradiction, which is impossible. And likewise for touch, taste, and smell: do we say 'I touched the non-existent' or 'I tasted it' or 'I smelled it'? If someone claimed that, could any rational person believe him? And know that this discussion reflects further clarification on the discussion of power and will and their not pertaining to other than possibles, for the principle in both is one.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage defines the third category of divine attributes: hearing (al-sam‘) and sight (al-basar). The author states that these attributes pertain (ta‘alluq) to existents (al-mawjūdāt), not to non-existents (al-ma‘dūmāt). He presents two scholarly positions: (1) both attributes encompass all existents in a comprehensive manner beyond mere knowledge, and (2) a view attributed to al-Sa‘d (likely al-Dashtaki or al-Taftazani) that hearing only relates to sounds and sight only to visible things, thus not all existents. The author delegates the exact reality to Allah, as many scholars do. The key agreed point is that these attributes do not attach to non-existents, because that would imply a contradiction—existence and non-existence cannot coexist. He illustrates with human senses (touch, taste, smell) to show absurdity. Finally, he connects this discussion to power (al-qudra) and will (al-irāda), noting the same principle: all these attributes pertain only to possible things (al-mumkināt).

Key Takeaway
Divine hearing and sight pertain exclusively to existents; any claim that they relate to non-existents entails a logical contradiction.

The Attribute of Life: A Foundational Attribute
Buti defines the attribute of life and its role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of life is unique in not relating to anything external and how it serves as a basis for other attributes.

Core Idea
Life is the attribute that makes all other attributes possible.

In this passage, Buti introduces the fourth division of divine attributes—the attribute of life. Unlike the attributes of disclosure and influence, life has no connection to anything outside God's essence; instead, it is the foundation upon which all other attributes depend. This lesson will help you see how life functions as a prerequisite for the other attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the precise wording.
Notice that the passage contrasts life with other attributes.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage states that life 'does not pertain to anything.' Understand that this means life has no direct relation to any external object.
Contrast with attributes like knowledge, which pertains to objects.
3
Compare with other attribute types
Buti distinguishes life from attributes of disclosure (kashf) and influence (ta'thir). Note how each type relates to external things, while life does not.
Attributes of disclosure include knowledge, hearing, sight; influence includes will and power.
4
Understand life's foundational role
The passage concludes that life validates (yusahhih) the subsistence of other attributes. Reflect on why life must exist first for other attributes to be present.
This makes life a prerequisite, not an active attribute.
5
Reflect on the overall significance
Consider why the author categorizes life separately. How does this affect our understanding of God's attributes?
This classification helps avoid misconceptions about God's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الرابع : فلا يتعلق بشيء ، وهو صفة الحياة . فهي بالنسبة الله تعالى قائمة بذاته لا تعلق لها بشيء سواه . إذ ليس لها علاقة بالأشياء لا على وجه الكشف كالعلم والسمع والبصر ، ولا على وجه التأثير والتخصيص كالإرادة والقدرة . وإنما هي معنى قائم بذات الله تعالى ، شأنه أن يصحح قيام تلك الصفات السابقة به
As for the fourth division: it does not pertain to anything, and it is the attribute of life. It, with respect to Allah Almighty, subsists in His essence, having no relation to anything other than Him. For it has no connection to things, neither in the way of disclosure like knowledge, hearing, and sight, nor in the way of influence and specification like will and power. Rather it is a meaning subsisting in Allah's essence, whose function is to validate the subsistence of those previous attributes in Him.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth category: the attribute of life (ṣifat al-ḥayāh). Unlike the previous attributes, life does not 'pertain' (tata‘allaq) to anything external. The author explains that life is a quality that subsists in God's essence (qā’im bi-dhātihi) and has no relation to things. He distinguishes it from two types of attributes: (1) those of disclosure (kashf) like knowledge, hearing, and sight, which relate to objects as known or perceived; and (2) those of influence and specification (ta’thīr wa takhṣīṣ) like will and power, which affect objects. Life is neither. Instead, it is a foundational meaning (ma‘nā) whose role is to validate (yuṣaḥḥiḥ) the subsistence of the other attributes—i.e., without life, the other attributes cannot exist in God.

Key Takeaway
Life is the foundational attribute that enables all other attributes to subsist in God, without itself relating to anything external.

The Five Consequences of Divine Attributes and the Necessity of Transcendence
Buti enumerates five consequences and begins explaining the first (transcendence).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five doctrinal consequences that follow from affirming divine attributes, and how belief in those attributes necessitates negating their opposites.

Core Idea
Affirming God’s attributes demands negating all opposites and imperfections.

After establishing the divine attributes through definitive proofs, Buti now draws out the doctrinal implications. He lists five key consequences that shape Islamic theology, beginning with the most fundamental: God’s absolute transcendence. This lesson presents the five consequences and the start of the first explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of five consequences
Carefully read the enumeration of the five doctrinal realities that follow from the attributes. Note their order.
The first is transcendence, which is the focus of the remaining explanation.
2
Understand the reasoning
Learn why belief in the attributes necessitates negating their opposites. The author explains that because attributes are proven by definitive evidence, believing in them requires removing any opposite.
This is a core principle of Islamic theology: affirmation of attributes implies negation of imperfection.
3
Identify the specific negations
Notice the negations listed: no partner, no place, no time, not a substance, accident, or body. These clarify that God is not like created things.
The text cuts off after 'it is not valid for Him', but the pattern is clear.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this teaching guards against anthropomorphism and establishes tanzih (transcendence) as a fundamental principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثا ما يترتب على هذه الصفات من الحقائق الاعتقادية وتتلخص هذه الحقائق في الأمور التالية : أولاً - تنزيه الله تعالى عن أضداد هذه الصفات وسائر النقائص . ثانياً - نفي العلة الغائية عن أفعاله جل جلاله . ثالثاً ـ لا يجب على الله لعباده أو لأحد من خلقه شيء والحسن والقبح أمر اعتباري . رابعاً - مصير الإرادة الإنسانية أمام إرادة الله جل جلاله . خامساً - القضاء والقدر : معناهما وضرورة الإيمان بهما ولنبدأ بأولها وهو : ۱ - تنزيه الله تعالى عن أضداد هذه الصفات وعن سائر النقائص وبيان ذلك أن الصفات التي فرغنا من شرحها وبيان ما يتعلق بها ، ثابتة الله تعالى بكل من دليلي النقل والعقل القاطعين ، كما قد رأيت، فلا بد من الإيمان بها بأن نستيقن اتصاف الله عز وجل بكل واحدة منها . والإيمان بها يقتضي سلب نقائض كل منها عن الله جل جلاله ، فالله عز وجل ، بموجب ثبوت تلك الصفات له ، ليس له شريك ولا ظهير ، ولا يتحيز في مكان ولا ينحصر في زمان ، ليس بجوهر ولا عرض ولا جسم ولا يصح عليه
Third: What follows from these attributes of doctrinal realities. These realities are summarized in the following matters: First – Transcendence of Allah Almighty from the opposites of these attributes and all imperfections. Second – Negation of a final cause from His actions, glorified be He. Third – Nothing is obligatory upon Allah toward His servants or any of His creation; goodness and evil are matters of convention. Fourth – The fate of the human will before the will of Allah, glorified be He. Fifth – Predestination and decree: their meaning and the necessity of believing in them. And we begin with the first of them, which is: 1 – Transcendence of Allah Almighty from the opposites of these attributes and from all imperfections. The explanation of that is that the attributes which we have finished explaining and showing what pertains to them are established for Allah Almighty by both definitive evidences of transmission and intellect, as you have seen. So it is necessary to believe in them by being certain of Allah's being described with each one of them. And belief in them necessitates negating the opposites of each of them from Allah, glorified be He. So Allah Almighty, by virtue of the establishment of those attributes for Him, has no partner nor helper, is not confined in a place, is not restricted in time, is not a substance, not an accident, not a body, and it is not valid for Him...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage lists five important doctrinal consequences (ḥaqā’iq i‘tiqādiyya) that follow from the divine attributes described earlier. The author enumerates them: (1) God’s transcendence above opposites (ḍiddād) and all imperfections; (2) negation of a final cause (al-‘illa al-ghā’iyya) in God’s actions; (3) nothing is obligatory upon God toward creation, and good and evil are conventional; (4) the relation between human will and God’s will; (5) predestination (al-qaḍā’) and decree (al-qadar)—their meaning and necessity of belief. Then he begins explaining the first: since the attributes are proven by definitive textual (al-naql) and rational (al-‘aql) evidence, we must believe in them, and this belief requires negating their opposites. He then lists negations: God has no partner or helper, is not confined in place or time, is not a substance (jawhar), an accident (‘araḍ), or a body (jism). The text cuts off at 'and it is not valid for Him...' (wa lā yaṣiḥḥu ‘alayhi). This underscores the Ash‘ari principle that affirming attributes does not imply anthropomorphism; rather, it demands transcendence.

القضاء والقدر
جوهر

جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming divine attributes implies that God has bodily parts or is confined in space/time.
Correction: On the contrary, affirming attributes necessarily requires negating all opposites, including any likeness to creation. God is not a body, substance, or accident.

Checkpoint
What does belief in the divine attributes necessitate regarding God?
A) Affirmation of bodily parts.
B) Negation of opposites of those attributes.
C) Belief that God has a place.
D) Denial of the attributes.

Key Takeaway
The first and foremost consequence of affirming divine attributes is God's absolute transcendence from all opposites and imperfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Examine your own mental image: is there any hidden assumption of spatiality or composition? What step will you take today to purify your conception of tanzīh?
?
The passage states that nothing is obligatory upon Allah toward His servants. Have you ever felt that Allah owes you something? A certain outcome, a specific treatment? Confront that expectation now. How does this teaching dissolve your sense of entitlement, and what would it cost you to surrender that feeling completely?

The Five Consequences of Belief in God's Attributes
Buti enumerates five doctrinal truths that follow from God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five doctrinal consequences that necessarily follow from affirming God's attributes, as enumerated by Buti.

Core Idea
God's attributes lead to five non-negotiable truths about transcendence, causality, obligation, human will, and destiny.

Having established the divine attributes, Buti now draws out their implications. He lists five key doctrinal truths that every Muslim must hold, ranging from God's transcendence to the reality of divine decree. Each consequence is a cornerstone of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the full list of five consequences without analysis. Notice the order and the thematic progression from transcendence to destiny.
The list is presented in a clear, numbered format.
2
Understand each item
For each of the five items, think about its meaning and why it follows from God's attributes. How does each truth protect tawhid?
3
Connect to the broader argument
Ask yourself: How does each consequence relate to the previous discussion of God's attributes? For example, tanzih is directly linked to the attribute of perfection.
This passage is the third section of a larger work.
4
Reflect on significance
Consider why these five are considered essential truths. What would be missing if any one of them were denied?
These consequences form the basis for further elaboration in the next lessons.

·
Third - Nothing is obligatory upon God for His servants or for anyone among His creation; and goodness and evil are a matter of consideration (i'tibari).
Fourth - The fate of human will before the will of God, may His glory be exalted
Fifth - Divine decree and destiny (al-qada wa al-qadr): their meaning and the necessity of believing in them
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration. The author states that the third section (ثالثا) is about what follows from these attributes concerning doctrinal truths. He then lists five items: first, transcendence (tanzih) of God from opposites and deficiencies; second, denial of final cause in God's acts; third, that nothing is obligatory upon God, and that moral values (husn and qubh) are relative (i'tibari); fourth, the relationship between human will and God's will; fifth, divine decree and destiny. He then signals that he will begin with the first item. The enumeration is complete and each item is a distinct theological claim that will be elaborated.

Correction: Buti specifies that goodness and evil are i'tibari (relative/considerative), meaning they are not objective obligations on God but are determined by His will.

Key Takeaway

?

Meaning of Surah in the Hadith of Adam's Creation
Explains that 'surah' means attribute, not physical form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'surah' (image) in the hadith refers to attributes (sifāt), not a physical form, and how this interpretation avoids anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
'Image' in the hadith means attribute, not body.

This short passage tackles a common question about a famous hadith: what does it mean that Adam was created 'upon the image of the Merciful'? The author clarifies that the word 'surah' here does not imply a physical likeness to God, but rather refers to attributes like knowledge and perception, which are from God's attributes but in a manner befitting Him. This interpretation protects God's transcendence while affirming the honor of Adam.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the author's explanation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author links the pronoun 'his' to either Adam or God, and then cites the version 'upon the image of the Merciful'.
2
Focus on the key phrase: 'al-sūrah bi-maʿnā al-ṣifah'
The author explicitly states that 'surah' means 'attribute' (sifah). This is the core teaching. Understand how this shifts the meaning from physical form to qualities.
This is the main point of the lesson.
3
Reflect on the attributes mentioned
The author specifies that Adam was equipped with attributes of knowledge and perception, which are from God's attributes. Think about how this honors Adam without implying similarity to God.
These attributes are given to Adam as a creature, not in the same way God possesses them.
4
Consider the theological implications
Appreciate that this interpretation avoids anthropomorphism (tashbīh). It affirms that Adam is honored without compromising God's transcendence.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology: affirming attributes without likening God to creation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي فليكرم الوجه الذي هو مظهر الخلقة آدم عليه الصلاة والسلام . أو الضمير عائد إلى ذات الله تعالى ، وذلك كما تدل عليه الرواية الثابتة الأخرى : ( إن الله خلق آدم على صورة الرحمن ) . ولكن الصورة بمعنى الصفة ، أي جهزه بصفات العلم والإدراك التي هي من صفات الله عز وجل .
i.e., let him honor the face which is the manifestation of creation, Adam, peace be upon him. Or the pronoun refers to the Essence of God Almighty, and that as indicated by the other established narration: 'Indeed, God created Adam upon the image of the Merciful.' But 'image' (al-ṣūrah) means attribute (al-ṣifah), i.e., He equipped him with attributes of knowledge and perception which are from the attributes of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of the hadith 'God created Adam upon the image of the Merciful.' The author begins by clarifying that the pronoun in 'his image' could refer to Adam himself (the face manifesting creation) or to God's Essence. He then cites the narration that explicitly says 'upon the image of the Merciful.' Crucially, he explains that 'image' here does not mean a physical form, but rather 'attribute' (ṣifah). Thus, God equipped Adam with attributes like knowledge and perception, which are from God's own attributes, but not in a literal sense that implies similarity. This interpretation avoids anthropomorphism while affirming the honor of Adam.

Correction: The author clarifies that 'surah' means 'attribute' (sifah), not physical form. Adam was given attributes like knowledge and perception that are from God's attributes, but not in a literal or physical sense.
