The First Opinion: Aisha's Denial of Seeing Allah
Aisha's denial of seeing Allah in this world with three Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first companion opinion that seeing Allah in this world is impossible, as championed by Aisha, and her three Quranic proofs.

Core Idea
Aisha used three Quranic verses to decisively deny that the Prophet saw Allah in this world.

The Companions differed on whether Allah can be seen in this world. The first view, led by Aisha, held that revelation only promises the vision in the Hereafter and that seeing Allah before death is impossible. She supported her position with three strong Quranic proofs. Let's examine her argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about Aisha's stance
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the strong language Aisha uses: 'Whoever tells you them has lied.'
2
Identify the three categories of lies
Aisha lists three types of false claims about the Prophet. Find each one and the corresponding Quranic verse she cites.
The three lies are: seeing Allah, knowing the future, and concealing revelation.
3
Understand the Quranic proofs
For each lie, note which verse(s) she uses. Reflect on why each verse refutes the claim.
For seeing: al-An'am 103 and al-Shura 51. For future: Luqman 34. For concealment: al-Ma'ida 67.
4
Note her conclusion about Jibril
Aisha states that the Prophet saw Jibril, not Allah, twice. This is her positive affirmation to counter the assumption of seeing Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنهم من قال لم يرد السمع إلا بما يدل على الرؤية في الآخرة فقط ، بل الذي جاء به السمع هو امتناع رؤية أحد من الناس ربه قبل الموت وذلك للحديث المرفوع الذي رواه مسلم عن عبادة بن الصامت أنه قال : « واعلموا أنكم لن تروا ربكم حتى تموتوا » . وزعيم القائلين بهذا الرأي من الصحابة السيدة عائشة رضي الله عنها ، فقد روى البخاري وغيره عن مسروق قال : قلت لعائشة رضي الله عنها : يا أماه هل رأى محمد ربه ؟ فقالت : لقد قف شعري مما قلت ، أين أنت من ثلاث من حدثكهن فقد كذب : من حدثك أن محمداً رأى ربه فقد كذب ، ثم قرأت : ( لا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَارَ وَهُوَ اللطيف الخَبِيرُ ) [الأنعام : ١٠٣ ] ( وما كانَ لِبَشَرِ أَنْ يُكَلِّمَهُ اللهُ إِلا وَحْيا أَوْ مِنْ وَراء حجاب ) [الشورى : ٥١] . ومن حدثك أنه يعلم ما في غد فقد كذب . ثم قرأت : ( وَمَا تُدْري نَفْسٌ ماذا تَكْسِبُ غِداً ) [لقمان : ٣٤ ] . ومن حدثك أنه كتم فقد كذب وقرأت قوله تعالى : ( يا أيها الرسولُ بَلِّغ ما أُنْزِلَ إِلَيْكَ ) [ المائدة : ٦٧] . ولكن رأى جبريل عليه السلام في صورته مرتين . وذهب الأكثرون إلى أنه قد دل السمع على جواز رؤية الله تعالى في دار الدنيا وزعيم القائلين بهذا هو عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنه ومعه جمهور الصحابة . ومن أهم أدلتهم على ذلك حديث الإسراء والمعراج وقول الله تعالى : ( وَما جَعَلْنَا الرُّؤْيا التي أرَيْناكَ إِلا فِتْنَة للناس ) [ الإسراء : ٦٠ ] ، وأحاديث كثيرة وردت في ذلك . وقد أنكر الفريق الأول صحة الاستدلال بهذه الآية : مستدلين بأن ( الرؤيا ) بالألف تطلق على الرؤيا المنامية لا الحقيقة . إلا أن الجواب على ذلك هو أن الرؤيا كما تطلق على رؤيا النوم فإنها تطلق أيضاً على رؤيا اليقظة دون تفريق . وذلك لقول الشاعر : فكبر للرؤيا وهش فؤاده وبشر قلباً كان جماً بلابله . وقالوا أيضاً : إنه تعالى سمى هذه الرؤيا فتنة للناس : أي ابتلاء واختباراً لهم وليس من المعقول أن يكون الحديث عن رؤيا في المنام سبب فتنة واختبار لإيمان الناس . هذا وما دامت المسألة مختلفاً فيها بين الصحابة أنفسهم : فليس ثمة ما يدعو في باب العقيدة - إلى الجزم بقول من القولين ، وإن كنا نرجح في ذلك مذهب الجمهور من الصحابة ثم من بعدهم من الأئمة والعلماء ، وهو أن السمع دلّ على إمكان رؤية الله عز وجل في الدنيا بل دلّ على وقوعها أيضاً من رسول الله . وهذا آخر ما يجب أن تعلمه من حقائق الإلهيات ، وجميعها مطوي في شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله التي هي الشطر الأول من الشهادتين اللتين بها يتم إسلام المسلم وإيمانه . أما الشطر الثاني ، وهو شهادة : أن محمداً رسول الله ، فينطوي تحته كل أبحاث النبوات : التي سنشرع في بيانها .
Some of them said that revelation only indicates seeing [Allah] in the afterlife only, but rather what revelation brought is the impossibility of any person seeing his Lord before death, due to the marfu' hadith that Muslim narrated from 'Ubada ibn al-Samit that he said: 'And know that you will not see your Lord until you die.' The leader of those who held this opinion among the Companions is Lady 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her. Al-Bukhari and others narrated from Masruq that he said: I said to 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her: O mother, did Muhammad see his Lord? She said: My hair stood on end from what you said. Where are you from three [matters]? Whoever tells you them has lied: Whoever tells you that Muhammad saw his Lord has lied, then she recited: (Vision comprehends Him not, and He comprehends vision, and He is the Subtle, the Aware) [al-An'am 103] and (It is not for any human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a veil) [al-Shura 51]. And whoever tells you that he knows what will be tomorrow has lied, then she recited: (And no soul knows what it will earn tomorrow) [Luqman 34]. And whoever tells you that he concealed [anything] has lied and she recited His saying: (O Messenger, convey that which has been sent down to you) [al-Ma'ida 67]. But he saw Jibril, peace be upon him, in his form twice. And the majority went that revelation has indicated the permissibility of seeing Allah, exalted, in the abode of this world, and the leader of those who hold this is 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, and with him is the multitude of the Companions. Among their most important evidences for that are the hadith of al-Isra' and al-Mi'raj and the saying of Allah, exalted: (And We made not the vision which We showed you except as a trial for mankind) [al-Isra' 60], and many hadiths narrated in that regard. The first group denied the validity of using this verse as evidence, arguing that 'al-ru'ya' (الرؤيا) with an alif applies to the dream vision, not reality. However, the response to that is that just as al-ru'ya applies to the vision of sleep, it also applies to the vision of wakefulness without distinction, due to the saying of the poet: 'He magnified Allah for the vision and his heart cheered, and he gave glad tidings to a heart whose calamities were many.' They also said: Indeed, He, exalted, called this vision a trial (fitna) for the people: i.e., an affliction and a test for them, and it is not reasonable that talk about a vision in a dream would be a cause of trial and test for people's faith. This, and as long as the matter is differed upon among the Companions themselves, there is nothing in the chapter of creed that calls for certainty regarding either of the two opinions, although we prefer in that the madhhab of the majority of the Companions and then after them of the imams and scholars, namely that revelation indicates the possibility of seeing Allah, mighty and majestic, in this world, rather it indicates its occurrence as well from the Messenger of Allah. And this is the last of what you must know of the realities of the divine matters (ilahiyyat), and all of them are encompassed in the testimony that there is no god but Allah, which is the first part of the two testimonies by which a Muslim's Islam and faith are completed. As for the second part, namely the testimony that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, under it are encompassed all the researches on prophethood (nubuwwat), which we shall begin to explain.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a combined presentation of a doctrinal dispute and the author's resolution. It begins by stating the two opinions among the Companions regarding seeing Allah in this world. The first opinion holds that revelation only speaks of the vision in the Hereafter and actually prohibits seeing Allah before death, citing a hadith from Ubada ibn al-Samit that one will not see his Lord until death. The leader of this view is Aisha, who vehemently denied that the Prophet saw his Lord, using three Quranic verses: the verse that vision does not comprehend Him (al-An'am 103), the verse that Allah only speaks to humans through revelation or behind a veil (al-Shura 51), and the verse that no soul knows what it will earn tomorrow (Luqman 34). She also recited the verse commanding the Prophet to convey everything (al-Ma'ida 67) to refute the claim that he concealed something. She concluded that the Prophet saw Jibril, not Allah, twice. The second opinion, held by the majority led by Ibn Abbas, states that revelation indicates the possibility of seeing Allah in this world. Their key evidence includes the hadith of Isra' and Mi'raj and the verse where Allah says He made the vision (ru'ya) a trial for people (al-Isra' 60). The first group objects that the word 'ru'ya' in that verse means a dream vision, not a waking one. The author responds by citing linguistic usage and poetry to show that 'ru'ya' applies to both sleep and wakeful visions. The first group further objects that calling it a 'fitna' (trial) would not make sense if it were merely a dream, but the author implies that it is indeed a trial, so the objection fails. The author then gives his conclusion: since the Companions themselves differed, one should not be dogmatic in matters of creed, but he personally prefers the majority view that revelation indicates the possibility and even actual occurrence of seeing Allah in this world for the Prophet. Finally, he transitions to the next topic, stating that this concludes the discussion of divine matters (ilahiyyat), which are summed up in the first shahada, and that the second shahada introduces the researches on prophethood, which will follow.

Key Terms
رؤية الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Aisha denied that Allah can be seen at all, even in the afterlife.
Correction: Aisha only denied seeing Allah in this world; she affirmed the vision in the Hereafter as indicated by revelation.

Checkpoint
According to Aisha, what is the third claim that is a lie about the Prophet?
A) That he saw his Lord
B) That he knows the future
C) That he concealed any revelation
D) That he saw Jibril twice

Key Takeaway
Aisha used three Quranic proofs to firmly deny that the Prophet saw Allah in this world, establishing the first companion opinion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Aisha's hair stood on end when asked if the Prophet saw Allah. When you hear a belief that contradicts clear revelation, does your heart react with such intensity? How do you cultivate that same reverence for the Qur'an's words?
?
Aisha used three clear proofs to refute a false claim. Are there areas in your faith where you passively accept ideas without checking them against the Qur'an? What is one such area you need to re-examine starting today?
?
The Prophet was not allowed to see Allah in this world, yet he saw Jibril twice. Are you content with the way Allah reveals Himself to you through His signs and scriptures, or do you secretly wish for a direct vision that would remove all doubt? What does that desire reveal about your current level of faith?

The Majority Opinion: Evidence for Seeing Allah
Presentation of the majority opinion and the debate over the ru'ya verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second companion opinion, led by Ibn Abbas, that seeing Allah in this world is possible, their key evidence from the Isra' and Mi'raj, and the objection that the ru'ya verse refers to a dream vision, along with the response.

Core Idea
The majority of Companions held that revelation indicates the possibility of seeing Allah in this world, with the Isra' verse as key evidence.

The second view, supported by the majority of Companions led by Ibn Abbas, holds that revelation permits seeing Allah in this world. Their strongest evidence is the hadith of Isra' and Mi'raj and the verse that calls the vision (ru'ya) a trial for people. However, the first group objected that the word ru'ya means a dream vision, not a waking one. The author provides a strong response. Let's examine this debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the presentation of the majority view
Read the Arabic passage describing the second opinion. Note that the leader is 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, and the evidence includes the hadith of Isra' and Mi'raj.
2
Identify the key evidence
The majority cites two main proofs: the hadith of Isra' and Mi'raj and the Quranic verse (al-Isra' 60) where Allah says He made the vision a trial for people.
The verse is: 'And We made not the vision which We showed you except as a trial for mankind.'
3
Understand the objection
The first group objects that the word 'ru'ya' in the verse specifically means a dream vision, not a waking vision, so it does not support seeing Allah in wakefulness.
They base this on the spelling with alif (الرؤيا) which is often used for dreams.
4
Follow the author's response
The author gives two responses: first, linguistically 'ru'ya' applies to both sleep and wakeful visions; second, the context of fitna (trial) shows it must be a waking vision to be a significant test.
He supports the first with a line of poetry and the second with logical reasoning.

Commentary
This passage is a combined presentation of a doctrinal dispute and the author's resolution. It begins by stating the two opinions among the Companions regarding seeing Allah in this world. The first opinion holds that revelation only speaks of the vision in the Hereafter and actually prohibits seeing Allah before death, citing a hadith from Ubada ibn al-Samit that one will not see his Lord until death. The leader of this view is Aisha, who vehemently denied that the Prophet saw his Lord, using three Quranic verses: the verse that vision does not comprehend Him (al-An'am 103), the verse that Allah only speaks to humans through revelation or behind a veil (al-Shura 51), and the verse that no soul knows what it will earn tomorrow (Luqman 34). She also recited the verse commanding the Prophet to convey everything (al-Ma'ida 67) to refute the claim that he concealed something. She concluded that the Prophet saw Jibril, not Allah, twice. The second opinion, held by the majority led by Ibn Abbas, states that revelation indicates the possibility of seeing Allah in this world. Their key evidence includes the hadith of Isra' and Mi'raj and the verse where Allah says He made the vision (ru'ya) a trial for people (al-Isra' 60). The first group objects that the word 'ru'ya' in that verse means a dream vision, not a waking one. The author responds by citing linguistic usage and poetry to show that 'ru'ya' applies to both sleep and wakeful visions. The first group further objects that calling it a 'fitna' (trial) would not make sense if it were merely a dream, but the author implies that it is indeed a trial, so the objection fails. The author then gives his conclusion: since the Companions themselves differed, one should not be dogmatic in matters of creed, but he personally prefers the majority view that revelation indicates the possibility and even actual occurrence of seeing Allah in this world for the Prophet. Finally, he transitions to the next topic, stating that this concludes the discussion of divine matters (ilahiyyat), which are summed up in the first shahada, and that the second shahada introduces the researches on prophethood, which will follow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many believe that 'ru'ya' (with alif) exclusively means a dream vision and cannot refer to waking vision.
Correction: Linguistically, 'ru'ya' applies to both dream and waking vision without distinction, as demonstrated by poetic usage.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that 'ru'ya' in al-Isra' 60 refers only to a dream vision?
A) He admits it is a dream vision but says it still proves the point.
B) He says the word applies to both sleep and wakeful visions.
C) He says the verse is metaphorical.
D) He says the hadith is stronger evidence.

Key Takeaway
The majority's evidence for seeing Allah in this world is strong, and the objection based on the word 'ru'ya' is refuted by linguistic and contextual arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the vision of Allah was made a trial for people, have you considered that your own encounters with divine truths—through revelation, nature, or life events—are also tests? Are you treating them as a trial that should deepen your faith, or as mere information?
?
The majority of Companions, including Ibn Abbas, held that seeing Allah in this world is possible. Do you trust their collective understanding more than your own presumptions? What personal assumption about Allah's transcendence might be preventing you from accepting what revelation teaches?
?
If the vision of Allah is not limited to the afterlife, does your heart long to see Him now? Or have you become so accustomed to 'seeing' through the veil of this world that you've stopped yearning for the real vision? What would change in your worship if you truly expected to see Him?

Author's Conclusion and Transition to Prophethood
Author's conclusion preferring the majority view and transition to prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's conclusion that the majority view is preferable, and how the discussion of divine matters (ilahiyyat) is encompassed in the first shahada, leading to the topic of prophethood.

Core Idea
The author prefers the majority view that seeing Allah in this world is possible and occurred for the Prophet, and then transitions to prophethood as the second shahada.

After presenting both opinions and the debate, the author gives his own conclusion. He notes that since the Companions themselves differed, one should not be dogmatic, but he clearly states his preference for the majority view. He then concludes the entire section on divine matters (ilahiyyat) by stating that all these truths are contained in 'La ilaha illallah', the first shahada, and then turns to the second shahada, which introduces the study of prophethood. Let's see his words.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's concluding remarks
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note the phrase 'وإن كنا نرجح' which signals his preference.
2
Understand his preference and reasoning
The author says that since the Companions differed, one should not be certain, but he personally prefers the majority view that revelation indicates the possibility and actual occurrence of seeing Allah for the Prophet.
His reasoning combines respect for disagreement with a clear scholarly judgment.
3
Note the transition to the next section
The author declares that this is the last of the divine matters (ilahiyyat), all encompassed in the first shahada. He then announces the next topic: prophethood, under the second shahada.
This transition shows the structure of the book: ilahiyyat followed by nubuwwat.

فمنهم من قال لم يرد السمع إلا بما يدل على الرؤية في الآخرة فقط ، بل الذي جاء به السمع هو امتناع رؤية أحد من الناس ربه قبل الموت وذلك للحديث المرفوع الذي رواه مسلم عن عبادة بن الصامت أنه قال : « واعلموا أنكم لن تروا ربكم حتى تموتوا » . وزعيم القائلين بهذا الرأي من الصحابة السيدة عائشة رضي الله عنها ، فقد روى البخاري وغيره عن مسروق قال : قلت لعائشة رضي الله عنها : يا أماه هل رأى محمد ربه ؟ فقالت : لقد قف شعري مما قلت ، أين أنت من ثلاث من حدثكهن فقد كذب : من حدثك أن محمداً رأى ربه فقد كذب ، ثم قرأت : ( لا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَارَ وَهُوَ اللطيف الخَبِيرُ ) [الأنعام : ١٠٣ ] ( وما كانَ لِبَشَرِ أَنْ يُكَلِّمَهُ اللهُ إِلا وَحْيا أَوْ مِنْ وَراء حجاب ) [الشورى : ٥١] . ومن حدثك أنه يعلم ما في غد فقد كذب . ثم قرأت : ( وَمَا تُدْري نَفْسٌ ماذا تَكْسِبُ غِداً ) [لقمان : ٣٤ ] . ومن حدثك أنه كتم فقد كذب وقرأت قوله تعالى : ( يا أيها الرسولُ بَلِّغ ما أُنْزِلَ إِلَيْكَ ) [ المائدة : ٦٧] . ولكن رأى جبريل عليه السلام في صورته مرتين . وذهب الأكثرون إلى أنه قد دل السمع على جواز رؤية الله تعالى في دار الدنيا وزعيم القائلين بهذا هو عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنه ومعه جمهور الصحابة . ومن أهم أدلتهم على ذلك حديث الإسراء والمعراج وقول الله تعالى : ( وَما جَعَلْنَا الرُّؤْيا التي أرَيْناكَ إِلا فِتْنَة للناس ) [ الإسراء : ٦٠ ] ، وأحاديث كثيرة وردت في ذلك . وقد أنكر الفريق الأول صحة الاستدلال بهذه الآية : مستدلين بأن ( الرؤيا ) بالألف تطلق على الرؤيا المنامية لا الحقيقة . إلا أن الجواب على ذلك هو أن الرؤيا كما تطلق على رؤيا النوم فإنها تطلق أيضاً على رؤيا اليقظة دون تفريق . وذلك لقول الشاعر : فكبر للرؤيا وهش فؤاده وبشر قلباً كان جماً بلابله . وقالوا أيضاً : إنه تعالى سمى هذه الرؤيا فتنة للناس : أي ابتلاء واختباراً لهم وليس من المعقول أن يكون الحديث عن رؤيا في المنام سبب فتنة واختبار لإيمان الناس . هذا وما دامت المسألة مختلفاً فيها بين الصحابة أنفسهم : فليس ثمة ما يدعو في باب العقيدة - إلى الجزم بقول من القولين ، وإن كنا نرجح في ذلك مذهب الجمهور من الصحابة ثم من بعدهم من الأئمة والعلماء ، وهو أن السمع دلّ على إمكان رؤية الله عز وجل في الدنيا بل دلّ على وقوعها أيضاً من رسول الله . وهذا آخر ما يجب أن تعلمه من حقائق الإلهيات ، وجميعها مطوي في شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله التي هي الشطر الأول من الشهادتين اللتين بها يتم إسلام المسلم وإيمانه . أما الشطر الثاني ، وهو شهادة : أن محمداً رسول الله ، فينطوي تحته كل أبحاث النبوات : التي سنشرع في بيانها .
Some of them said that revelation only indicates seeing [Allah] in the afterlife only, but rather what revelation brought is the impossibility of any person seeing his Lord before death, due to the marfu' hadith that Muslim narrated from 'Ubada ibn al-Samit that he said: 'And know that you will not see your Lord until you die.' The leader of those who held this opinion among the Companions is Lady 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her. Al-Bukhari and others narrated from Masruq that he said: I said to 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her: O mother, did Muhammad see his Lord? She said: My hair stood on end from what you said. Where are you from three [matters]? Whoever tells you them has lied: Whoever tells you that Muhammad saw his Lord has lied, then she recited: (Vision comprehends Him not, and He comprehends vision, and He is the Subtle, the Aware) [al-An'am 103] and (It is not for any human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a veil) [al-Shura 51]. And whoever tells you that he knows what will be tomorrow has lied, then she recited: (And no soul knows what it will earn tomorrow) [Luqman 34]. And whoever tells you that he concealed [anything] has lied and she recited His saying: (O Messenger, convey that which has been sent down to you) [al-Ma'ida 67]. But he saw Jibril, peace be upon him, in his form twice. And the majority went that revelation has indicated the permissibility of seeing Allah, exalted, in the abode of this world, and the leader of those who hold this is 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, and with him is the multitude of the Companions. Among their most important evidences for that are the hadith of al-Isra' and al-Mi'raj and the saying of Allah, exalted: (And We made not the vision which We showed you except as a trial for mankind) [al-Isra' 60], and many hadiths narrated in that regard. The first group denied the validity of using this verse as evidence, arguing that 'al-ru'ya' (الرؤيا) with an alif applies to the dream vision, not reality. However, the response to that is that just as al-ru'ya applies to the vision of sleep, it also applies to the vision of wakefulness without distinction, due to the saying of the poet: 'He magnified Allah for the vision and his heart cheered, and he gave glad tidings to a heart whose calamities were many.' They also said: Indeed, He, exalted, called this vision a trial (fitna) for the people: i.e., an affliction and a test for them, and it is not reasonable that talk about a vision in a dream would be a cause of trial and test for people's faith. This, and as long as the matter is differed upon among the Companions themselves, there is nothing in the chapter of creed that calls for certainty regarding either of the two opinions, although we prefer in that the madhhab of the majority of the Companions and then after them of the imams and scholars, namely that revelation indicates the possibility of seeing Allah, mighty and majestic, in this world, rather it indicates its occurrence as well from the Messenger of Allah. And this is the last of what you must know of the realities of the divine matters (ilahiyyat), and all of them are encompassed in the testimony that there is no god but Allah, which is the first part of the two testimonies by which a Muslim's Islam and faith are completed. As for the second part, namely the testimony that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, under it are encompassed all the researches on prophethood (nubuwwat), which we shall begin to explain.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a combined presentation of a doctrinal dispute and the author's resolution. It begins by stating the two opinions among the Companions regarding seeing Allah in this world. The first opinion holds that revelation only speaks of the vision in the Hereafter and actually prohibits seeing Allah before death, citing a hadith from Ubada ibn al-Samit that one will not see his Lord until death. The leader of this view is Aisha, who vehemently denied that the Prophet saw his Lord, using three Quranic verses: the verse that vision does not comprehend Him (al-An'am 103), the verse that Allah only speaks to humans through revelation or behind a veil (al-Shura 51), and the verse that no soul knows what it will earn tomorrow (Luqman 34). She also recited the verse commanding the Prophet to convey everything (al-Ma'ida 67) to refute the claim that he concealed something. She concluded that the Prophet saw Jibril, not Allah, twice. The second opinion, held by the majority led by Ibn Abbas, states that revelation indicates the possibility of seeing Allah in this world. Their key evidence includes the hadith of Isra' and Mi'raj and the verse where Allah says He made the vision (ru'ya) a trial for people (al-Isra' 60). The first group objects that the word 'ru'ya' in that verse means a dream vision, not a waking one. The author responds by citing linguistic usage and poetry to show that 'ru'ya' applies to both sleep and wakeful visions. The first group further objects that calling it a 'fitna' (trial) would not make sense if it were merely a dream, but the author implies that it is indeed a trial, so the objection fails. The author then gives his conclusion: since the Companions themselves differed, one should not be dogmatic in matters of creed, but he personally prefers the majority view that revelation indicates the possibility and even actual occurrence of seeing Allah in this world for the Prophet. Finally, he transitions to the next topic, stating that this concludes the discussion of divine matters (ilahiyyat), which are summed up in the first shahada, and that the second shahada introduces the researches on prophethood, which will follow.

Key Terms
شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله
شهادة أن محمداً رسول الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the author is non-committal or agnostic on this issue because he says 'not to be certain'.
Correction: The author says not to be dogmatic since the Companions differed, but he clearly states his preference for the majority view that seeing Allah in this world is possible and occurred for the Prophet.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between the divine matters (ilahiyyat) and the first shahada?
A) The divine matters are separate from the shahada.
B) All divine matters are encompassed in 'La ilaha illallah'.
C) The divine matters are only explained in the second shahada.
D) The shahada is not related to the divine matters discussed.

Key Takeaway
The author concludes that the majority view is preferable, and all divine truths are contained in the first shahada, setting the stage for prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the author prefers the view that seeing Allah in this world is possible and occurred for the Prophet. Do you truly believe that you will see Allah in the Hereafter? Examine your heart: does this hope bring you real joy and motivate your obedience, or is it a distant thought with no emotional weight? What step will you take today to make the vision of Allah your greatest aspiration?
?
The author says all divine truths are contained in 'La ilaha illallah'. When you recite this phrase, do you feel the weight of its meaning? Is there any area of your life where you still act as if something else—money, status, comfort—deserves your ultimate trust? Identify one such area and make a firm intention to purify your tawheed in that aspect starting now.
?
This passage marks a turning point from belief in Allah to belief in His Messenger. How does your acceptance of Muhammad as the final messenger shape your daily decisions? Are there any sunnah practices you have neglected or dismissed? Commit to reviving one specific sunnah this week as a practical manifestation of your second shahada.
